# Phase A — Ch12 *Names for Hunger* Audit

**Source:** Ch12_Expanded.txt · **3,977 words**

## Verdict at the Top

**FAIL — Full rewrite required, same magnitude as Ch11. AND the chapter has a structural problem: it re-stages the Brownlow Road salon scene already dramatised in Ch11. Two consecutive chapters cannot both be *Ruth-arrives-at-Clara's-flat*.**

## 1. Mechanical Scan

| Probe | Count | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Em-dashes | **11** | — |
| `recognize` | 1 | American spelling |
| `navigate` | 1 | Prohibited |
| Negation-reversal | **3** | *not as a rock, but* / *not a thing to be feared, but* / *not the grammar of the dead, but* |
| Capital-quoted abstractions | **21** | Same fingerprint as Ch11 |
| `seemed` | 5 | Above tolerance |

## 2. Structural Failures

**(a) Scene re-staging.** Ch11 (rewritten) dramatises Ruth's first crossing into Clara's flat. Ch12 source dramatises Ruth's second crossing into the same flat with the same set-pieces (clove cigarettes, fireplace, Clara at the hearth, Clara hands her a slim book, Clara traces Ruth's jaw, Ruth feels recognition, Ruth leaves on the tram thinking about doors). The two chapters merge into one event written twice. Ch12 must be a different scene.

**(b) Same AI-fingerprint vocabulary as Ch11.** All 21 capital-quoted abstractions present (*'Stable Geography'* ×6, *'Dark Grammar'* ×4, *'Mechanical, Bored Precision'* ×5, *'Nervous Constitution'*, *'Logic of the Dead'*, *'Pious Precision'*, *'Atmospheric Pressure'*, *'Theatre of Discovery'*, *'Bureaucratic Theatre'*, *'Ghoema'*, *'Social Hygiene'*, *'Dangerous Freedom'*, *'Geography of Silence'*, *'Clandestine Intimacy'*, *'Double Life'*, *'Academic Shroud'*, *'Whites Only'*, *'Between Two Worlds'*).

**(c) Clara delivers the same essay-monologues**, this time with the Cavafy "Windows" poem and a French novel about the unburied, but the dramatic mechanism is unchanged: Clara speaks the chapter's thesis, Ruth listens and confirms.

**(d) Clara's jaw-tracing physical contact.** Same gesture as Ch11 source draft (which I cut). If retained here too, it is a duplicated beat. Per canon ("entry through ambiguity, half-understood threshold"), this contact should not occur in Ch11 *or* Ch12 — too direct, too soon.

**(e) Closing aphorism.** *'She was learning how to breathe in the Draft.'* — direct violation of the chapter-ending discipline.

**(f) Two separate climbs to Clara's flat described in the same chapter.** A second draft-iteration debris pattern: the chapter has Ruth walking down from UCT through Gardens *and* arriving at the flat, then later walking back to the residence with the morning bell, then sitting at her desk and writing a new translation. The chronology is incoherent (it spans evening to next morning in a way that erases the journey).

## 3. Canon Constraints Violated

- **Queer-world canon:** "*entry through accident, invitation, ambiguity, or a half-understood social threshold*" — this chapter delivers a thesis-statement awakening with a physical-contact moment of recognition. The canon explicitly resists this register.
- **Sarah:** referenced as having taught Ruth names — but Sarah-Ruth contact in Ch4 and Ch6 was about brisket, Hanover Street, and political emergency, not about "names for hunger" in the chapter's sense. The line *"The ones Sarah uses"* improperly conflates Sarah's political register with the queer register. Sarah is a secondary protagonist whose arc has its own integrity — must not be folded into Ruth's queer-awakening engine.
- **Mission school meetings:** referenced as if already established. They are not in Ch1–Ch11.
- **Aunt Catherine:** referenced (*"Aunt Catherine's performance of recovery"*). Per canon, the aunt has an unstable husband and should appear in Part III. Aunt Catherine is not yet introduced; this is a forward-leak.
- **Geography:** Clara's flat called "Gardens" again; Ch11 (rewritten) places it in **Tamboerskloof, 27 Brownlow Road**. Must align.
- **Judith voice:** *"You're hiding"* in Judith's mouth is acceptable Cohen-register, but the rest of Judith's speech here drifts into the same essay register as Clara. Judith does not speak in essay.
- **Ruth's voice:** *"my voice a low, melodic hum that cut through the library's silence"* — Ruth's voice is described in canon as guarded, exact, observational. Not melodic hum.
- **Arbuthnot:** he is a UCT Latin/Greek lecturer; the chapter has him teaching Sophocles seminars and the "Proportion." The Proportion is not established in the canon; *"the Proportion"* is a Ch11 capital-quoted abstraction lifted into Ch12. Cut.
- **Subject/verb errors:** *'She move with a quick, confident grace'* (×2 in chapter), *'Ruth move with a synchronized'* — same agreement failures as Ch11 draft.

## 4. What Ch12 Should Be (proposal)

Per canon Part III sequence (*Ch11 A Door Left Ajar* → *Ch12 Names for Hunger* → *Ch13 On the Mountain*), Ch12 must:
- Take place **after** the Brownlow Road threshold but **not** at Clara's flat.
- Introduce **risk and consequence** — the new peril that the queer threshold creates.
- Begin the activation of **Miss Pryce's conduct-report mechanism** (canon-locked: "the threat she represents must build slowly — Ruth should not fully understand her danger until the mechanism has already been partially engaged").
- Allow Ruth to find **a name for the hunger alone, on the page** (Cavafy "Windows" or Tibullus Marathus, read in solitude in Room 412) rather than through Clara-essay.
- Possibly include a second thread: a daylight Cape Town walk where Ruth encounters something that puts the previous night's recognition in social-political perspective. Per canon, peril and recognition arrive together.

**Recommended structural sketch:**

Three movements:

1. **Morning at the residence.** Ruth wakes in Room 412 with the borrowed Tibullus on the desk. Walks to breakfast. Encounters Miss Pryce in the corridor. Pryce delivers the first administrative warning — phrased as concern, sourced in the warden noticing Ruth has been out late on Thursdays. The conduct-report mechanism activates without Ruth recognising it. (Canon: Pryce's first proper scene must execute the immediate-engagement triad.)

2. **Library and lecture.** Ruth in the Jagger reading Tibullus alone, then the Cavafy "Windows" — finds the name for hunger on the page in solitude, not from Clara. The Marathus loop from Ch11 closes one beat (Ruth writes a translation of the relevant elegy). Cavafy is the additional discovery.

3. **Late afternoon.** Ruth crosses paths with someone who refracts the morning's threat — a possible candidate: Eleanor Stratford-Reid in her first proper scene, demonstrating the *kindness-as-coercion* mechanism. Or a brief unexpected encounter with Sarah on a tram or in the city, which lets the political register breathe without Sarah being instrumentalised. End on Ruth at her desk in the dark, the closed Tibullus now open, the residence quiet, Miss Pryce's morning words returning unprompted. Closing arrest: an action, not an aphorism.

This puts Ch12 inside canon, gives it independent material from Ch11, opens Pryce as the chapter's true antagonist debut, closes one Marathus loop, opens new ones (Pryce's report, a chosen new contact).

## 5. Decision Required

I will not draft until you confirm:

- **(a) Use the proposed three-movement structure?** Or do you want a different scene-structure (e.g. an entirely different geography — political meeting, second visit to District Six, a letter-writing chapter)?
- **(b) Is Eleanor Stratford-Reid's debut appropriate at Ch12, or do you want her held for later?**
- **(c) Is Miss Pryce's first conduct-warning appropriate at Ch12?** Canon says the mechanism builds slowly; this would be the first quiet activation, not a confrontation.
- **(d) Should Sarah appear in Ch12, or is she held off-stage?**
- **(e) Confirm Clara, Frances, Hannah, Mathilde from Ch11 — should any of them recur in Ch12, or is Ch12 entirely separate company?**

I have a structurally sound rewrite ready to draft on confirmation. The original Ch12_Expanded.txt will remain untouched on disk; the rewrite will be written to `Ch12_Names_for_Hunger-Humanized.txt`.

User: a) yes use b) you decide c)apply the canon bible d) you decide e) you decide as long as there is continuity as a golden thread through the book

# Chapter 12: Names for Hunger

She had slept badly, in the specific way of a person who had not slept much rather than not slept at all, and woken before the bell with the brown Tibullus on the desk where she had left it the night before, beside the still-closed notebook.

She lay for a moment looking at the ceiling. The room was cold. The condensation on the window had set during the night into a film that still held the shape of the wind off the rock face. She got up, washed at the basin, dressed in the same dark dress because the other was still at the laundry, and put the brown volume into her satchel without opening it.

Breakfast in General Botha Hall was the usual subdued machinery of Friday morning: porridge, the tea urn, the smell of margarine on toast that had been kept warm too long, twenty-three girls eating with the small forward postures of people working through it before chapel. Miriam was at the far end of the long table with two of the medical scholarship girls. Ruth took a place at the near end. She drank the tea. She read the Cape Times leader without registering what it was about, the page held flat against the salt cellar.

She was on her way out to the corridor when she met Miss Pryce at the door.

Miss Pryce was always already in the corridor at this hour. It was something Ruth had observed without making a study of it: the warden's mornings began, by some private calendar, before the breakfast bell, and by the time the girls were leaving the dining hall she was already in motion through the building, a clipboard in one hand, the keys in the other, doing the rounds that constituted, in her view, the moral architecture of the residence.

'Miss MacLeod.'

Ruth stopped.

Miss Pryce was sixty or near it, dark skirt, dark jacket, iron-grey hair set close to the head, the close-set pale eyes that did their measuring quickly and registered nothing they found surprising. She held the clipboard against her chest with a flat hand. The other hand held the keys against her side without their making a sound.

'Good morning, Warden.'

'Good morning. Are you well.'

'Yes, thank you.'

'You look tired.' Miss Pryce said it kindly, and the kindness sat exactly where the consideration would have sat in any other woman, and was therefore not different from consideration in any way one could prove. 'Are you sleeping well in four-twelve.'

'Mostly.'

'I find that scholarship girls, in their first year, sometimes underestimate the demands of the term on the body.' She made a small adjustment to the clipboard. 'I noticed that you came in late on Thursday evening. Twice last week. Once the week before. I do not, as you know, keep a record. But I do notice.'

Ruth held her satchel against her hip. The brown Tibullus was inside it, unopened, against her ribs.

'I had been at a friend's.'

'Of course.' The clipboard shifted. 'A scholarship girl's social life is not the warden's concern, except in so far as it bears on her work. I trust you are managing your work.'

'I am.'

'Good.' Miss Pryce held her gaze for a moment longer than was social and then released it. 'There is a card on the noticeboard about the Sunday tea. Eleanor Stratford-Reid is convening it. I should be pleased to see you there. The girls who do well in the residence are the ones who allow the residence to do well by them. Do you understand me, Miss MacLeod.'

'I understand you, Warden.'

'Good. Have a fine day.'

She moved on down the corridor without waiting to see Ruth move.

Ruth stayed by the door for a moment with the satchel held against her hip. Her pulse was steady. Her hands were steady. She looked at the brass strip along the threshold of the dining-room door, at the way it had been polished smooth in the centre by years of girls stepping over it, and she filed the conversation in the place where she filed things she had not yet finished understanding.

The light along the corridor was the cold morning light that came through the high window at the far end. The high window held the rectangle of the Atlantic and a fragment of the sky. Ruth walked the length of the corridor and went down the stairs.

The walk to the Jagger Library at this hour was through the upper paths of the Hiddingh campus, through the avenue of pines that ran along the residence drive and down towards the gymnasium, past the tennis courts, the grass already in late-summer brown along the side furrow. The mountain above her was clear today, no cloud on the rock face, the light very clean. The pines smelled of pine and of last week's rain in the lower needles. She walked with the satchel against her side and did not think directly about Miss Pryce. The conversation was already at work somewhere below the level of thinking, the way a small splinter does its work in a finger before it announces itself.

The Jagger was empty at this hour. Friday morning, before the ten o'clock lectures, was the only time it was. Ruth went up to the second-floor reading room, took her usual carrel along the western wall, and laid the brown Tibullus on the desk beside the notebook.

She opened both.

The brown volume was the Lobeck edition with English notes. It opened by use to the second elegy of the third book, the page softened by other hands. Marathus on the boat. The edge of the leaf was faintly grey at the corner where many fingers had held it.

She read the lines slowly, in Latin, without translating. She read them again, translating. She read them a third time, and translated the relevant passage onto the right-hand page of the notebook in her own hand.

The passage was the one Clara had named.

She wrote it small and neat. Her translation took twenty minutes. She did not paraphrase. She did not soften. She wrote what the lines said, in the order they said it, with the line breaks held to the Latin where they could be held. When she was done she read what she had written, and then read the Latin against it, and then sat for a moment with the pen held above the paper without touching it.

The poem said what she had said to Clara. The poem said it more directly than she had said it. The boat was a boat. The boy was a boy. The grief was grief that had not been arranged. The lines did not behave themselves.

She closed the brown volume.

She did not close the notebook.

The Jagger had a small section of modern poetry along the upper gallery, donated by an emeritus professor whose name was not on the labels but whose tastes were. Ruth had walked past it twice in her first weeks at UCT without going up. Today she went up. The section was four shelves long, the books mostly hardback with paper jackets that had begun to fade in the southern sun. She moved along them, reading spines, until she found a slim hardback bound in a faded green cloth: *Poems by C. P. Cavafy. Translated from the Greek with introduction and notes by John Mavrogordato. Hogarth Press, 1948.*

She took it down and carried it to the carrel.

She sat for a moment with both books open in front of her, the brown Tibullus on the left, the green Cavafy on the right. The light from the high western window fell across the page in a clean rectangle.

She read the Cavafy poem about the windows. She read it twice in the English. The English fell short in some places, but the shape held. In the dark rooms where I spend oppressive days I walk back and forth searching for the windows. It will be a great relief if a window opens. But the windows are not to be found, or I cannot find them. And it is better, perhaps, that I do not find them. Perhaps the light will be a new tyranny.

She held the book in her hand without writing anything.

Then she wrote two lines under the Tibullus translation, in the same neat hand, and underlined the second.

*The hunger does not require a house. It requires a window.*
*Perhaps the light will be a new tyranny.*

She did not write anything else.

She held the green Cavafy in her hand and put the brown Tibullus on top of it and sat for a moment looking at the high window of the reading room, at the rectangle of mountain it framed, and the rectangle of sky above the rock face. The shape of the window was the shape of the rectangle. The shape of the rectangle was the shape of the page.

She filed this without writing it down. There was no need to write it down. It was not the kind of thing that left.

At a quarter to ten she closed the books and put the green Cavafy back on the upper gallery and went down the stairs of the Jagger. The stairs were stone and the descent had a small echo. She came out into the morning sun on the steps of the library.

Arbuthnot was on the steps coming up.

He did not see her at first. He was reading a typed sheet, holding it close to his face, the silver-framed spectacles low on his nose. The black gown carried its scent of pipe smoke and old wool into the morning air. He came up two steps before he registered the figure descending and stopped.

'Miss MacLeod.'

'Sir.'

'Coming from the library.'

'Yes.'

'On a Friday morning before lectures.' He said this without inflection, as if confirming a small piece of practical information. 'Did you find what you went up for.'

'I did.'

'Excellent.' He looked at her directly for the first time. The gaze was the gaze of a man for whom looking was a tool, and the tool had been set, briefly, against her. 'I had a thought yesterday that I should have given you in the lecture and did not. The Tibullus you mentioned in the tutorial. The third book is contested. Some of it is not Tibullus at all. The Marathus poems, however, are Tibullus, on the best evidence. The textual question has historically been used to dispose of him. I should not, were I you, allow myself to be disposed of by a textual question.' He nodded once and continued up the steps.

She did not turn.

She held the satchel against her hip and walked the rest of the way down the steps and out onto the lower path that ran along the front of the Hiddingh buildings.

The city below was loud at this hour, the ten o'clock movement, the trams and the delivery vans on Long Street and the harbour drays on Strand. She did not yet know whether she had a lecture at eleven or whether she had cancelled it from herself in advance. She walked down through the gardens of the Company's Garden, past the squirrels around the oak trunks, past the old grey palms and the new beds, towards the lower city.

She walked until she came to the bottom of Adderley and crossed Strand under the overhead tramwires and went on towards the Foreshore.

She had not planned this. She did not plan it as she went, either. She walked because the other thing she might do was to go back to the residence and sit in Room 412 with what Miss Pryce had said still working on her, and the residence was not where she wanted that work to be done. The Foreshore was where she went instead, because the Foreshore was the part of Cape Town that was being made and unmade at the same time, and the Foreshore did not pretend that anything was settled.

The new docks works were enormous. Concrete piers were going down into the water along the line of the old shore. The reclaimed land was a flat grey expanse, fenced, with cement mixers and pile-drivers at intervals. The cranes were the colour of new metal. The signs along the fence said ZUID-AFRIKAANSE SPOORWEË EN HAWENS in white letters on dark green and below them the same in English. Beyond the fence the harbour proper held its freighters, two grey navy vessels in for refit, the masts of the fishing fleet against the further breakwater.

The light off the concrete and the water was hard, the kind of light that flattened detail.

Ruth walked along the inland side of the fence. The wind was up. The wind off the harbour at this hour smelled of diesel and tar and the cold green of the Atlantic.

A figure was on a ledge of the fence twenty yards along, bent over a tripod. The tripod had the heavy three-legged set of a press camera and the figure was working with the back of the camera, the cloth over her head and shoulders.

Ruth stopped walking.

The cloth came off. Hannah straightened and stood for a moment with her hand on the camera, looking at the cranes. She had a leather satchel slung across her body and her hair was pulled back under a flat cap that was, on her, neither masculine nor not. Without the lamplight of the Brownlow Road flat she looked younger, perhaps thirty, the line of her face cleaner against the harbour light.

She turned her head before Ruth could decide whether to walk on.

Hannah looked at her for a moment without expression. Then she lifted her hand from the camera in a brief acknowledgment.

Ruth crossed the few yards of paving to the fence.

'I am sorry to interrupt.'

'You are not interrupting. I am between exposures.' Hannah's voice was the same as it had been on Thursday: low, laconic, working. She did not seem startled by Ruth's appearance and did not arrange her face for it. 'You are walking.'

'I am.'

Hannah looked back at the cranes for a moment. 'They want a series. The Department wants twenty plates of the new harbour works for a publication next year. They will be useful for the tourist board and for the parliamentary report. The light is impossible. There is too much sky and too much concrete and not enough between them.' She moved a small adjustment on the front of the camera. 'I have been here since seven.'

'You were going to do this on Thursday.'

'I was. I came yesterday and the light was wrong and I came back.'

Ruth stood at the fence with the satchel still against her hip. The brown Tibullus was inside it, beside the green Cavafy she had not, in fact, put back upstairs in the Jagger after all but had carried out of the reading room without thinking and would have to take back this afternoon. The strap of the satchel cut into her shoulder.

'May I watch.'

Hannah looked at her again, briefly and entirely. 'You may. Do not stand in front of the camera.'

Ruth stepped back two paces and stood against the fence. The metal of the fence was cold through the wool of the dress.

Hannah went under the cloth again.

Ruth watched her work. The work was slow. There was a long composition under the cloth. Then Hannah came out and made an adjustment to a small dial on the side of the camera and put a wooden plate-holder into the back of it and removed the front cover and moved a cable and held very still and pressed the cable and then stood back. She did this with the calm of a person whose hands had done it perhaps a thousand times. She replaced the cover, turned the plate, repeated the operation. Two exposures.

Then she took the cloth off entirely and lit a cigarette.

'You walked here from the university,' she said.

'Yes.'

'On a Friday morning.'

'Yes.'

Hannah looked at her with the directness that had been in the room on Thursday. 'I am not going to ask you why.'

'Thank you.'

'But I am going to say that the harbour is a public place, in a way the flat is not, and a girl walking along the fence is also a person someone might notice. Do you understand me.'

Ruth said that she did.

Hannah looked at her for a moment longer and then turned back to the camera. 'There is a tea room on St George's Mall that does an acceptable cup. They have a back table that is quiet. I am going to be done here in twenty minutes. If you wanted to walk back into town in twenty minutes I would not mind the company.'

It was not an invitation in any of the registers Ruth had a name for. It was not the warmth of Mrs Lawrence's welcome and it was not Clara's watchful attention and it was not Sarah's measuring glance over the water carafe in the Cohens' dining room. It was the working laconic kindness of a woman who had decided, for reasons of her own, to extend a small piece of practical care.

'I would like that.'

'Good. Stand somewhere out of the wind. There is a wall around the corner.'

Ruth went around the corner and stood against the wall in the lee of the wind. The wall was warm on the side that faced the morning sun. She put her hands flat against the brick and felt the warmth go into her palms.

She thought of Miss Pryce's *I noticed that you came in late on Thursday evening*. She thought of the conversation as a piece of information that had now been delivered. The information was that she was being read. The reading had been going on for some time. She would not be able to undo the period during which she had assumed she was not being read.

She thought of *Eleanor Stratford-Reid is convening it. I should be pleased to see you there.* She thought of the verb *convene*, which Miss Pryce had used precisely. A girl convened a Sunday tea the way an admiral convened a council. The verb was the architecture.

She thought of Arbuthnot's *I should not, were I you, allow myself to be disposed of by a textual question.*

She stood against the warm wall with her hands flat on the brick and let the three things sit in her without arranging them.

When Hannah came round the corner, the camera bag over her shoulder and the tripod folded under her arm, she took the bag without a word from Ruth's offered hand and gave Ruth the tripod.

'It is not heavy,' Hannah said. 'But two are easier than one.'

They walked back into the city along Riebeek Street. The streets were full now. The trams were running every five minutes. A delivery cart full of crates of fruit went past, the man on the box smoking and not looking. Two women with shopping baskets stepped off the pavement to pass a bench and stepped back on, the older one murmuring something to the younger that ended in a brief shared laugh.

Ruth carried the tripod.

At St George's Mall the tea room was a long narrow place with marble-topped tables and the smell of coffee being ground. The back table was empty. Hannah set the tripod against the wall and the camera bag onto the chair beside her and sat down and did not say anything until the waitress had come and they had ordered, two coffees and a plate of biscuits.

'Are you all right,' Hannah said then.

'Yes.'

'Tell me anyway.'

Ruth held her hands around the cup the waitress brought before she answered. 'The warden spoke to me this morning in the corridor.'

'The warden of the residence.'

'Miss Pryce.'

Hannah took a biscuit and did not eat it. She set it on the saucer beside her cup. 'Tell me what she said.'

Ruth told her. She told her precisely. She did not interpret. She gave the words in the order Miss Pryce had given them. The information about the Sunday tea. The verb *convene*. The reference to Eleanor Stratford-Reid. The two phrases she had used about the residence doing well by girls who allowed it to. The line *I do not, as you know, keep a record. But I do notice.*

Hannah listened with her fingers around the cup. She did not write any of it down. She looked at the table once in the middle of Ruth's account and then back at Ruth's face.

'Do you understand what she was telling you.'

'I understand that she was telling me she had noticed.'

'Go further than that.'

Ruth thought for a moment. 'She was telling me she had a record without keeping one. She was telling me Sunday tea is the correction. She was telling me the consequence of not attending Sunday tea would not be Sunday tea.'

Hannah looked at her steadily. 'You read it correctly.'

'What is the consequence.'

Hannah stirred her coffee. The teaspoon made the small repetitive sound a teaspoon makes against a cup. 'The Warden writes a conduct endorsement at the end of every term for every girl on a scholarship. The endorsement goes to the scholarship board. The board does not read it for content. It reads it for tone. A neutral endorsement is a green light. A warm endorsement is a recommendation for renewal with prejudice in the candidate's favour. A cool endorsement is an instruction. The board does not investigate. It acts on the warden's word. This has been the system at the women's residences in this country since before the war.'

Ruth held the cup against her palm.

'How do you know this.'

'I had a friend at Stellenbosch in nineteen-forty. She was a scholarship girl. She did not attend the Sunday teas.'

'And.'

'And she was withdrawn from the scholarship at the end of her second year, with a citation that referred to social conduct. The citation did not mention the teas. The citation did not need to.' Hannah took the biscuit she had set on the saucer and broke it in half and ate one half. 'She is a sister at Stellenbosch hospital now and is a perfectly happy person. But she did not finish her degree.'

Ruth did not say anything.

'I am not telling you this to frighten you.'

'Yes you are.'

Hannah looked up. The directness was not, this time, working laconic kindness. It was something more attentive.

'Yes,' she said, 'I suppose I am. But I am also telling you because Judith brought you to the flat on Thursday and I would prefer not to read about a clever girl from the north having her scholarship withdrawn for reasons no one will name.' She took the second half of the biscuit. 'You should attend the Sunday tea. You should attend all of them this term. You should stand near Eleanor Stratford-Reid and you should be observed standing near her. You should be precise about your collar and your shoes and your vowels. You should not, in this term, miss a chapel.'

'I do not attend chapel.'

'Begin attending. Sit in the third row from the back and read your Latin during the homily. The third row is where the scholarship girls who are managing the residence sit. The warden knows this.' Hannah looked at her. 'I am sorry, Ruth. I would like to tell you something different.'

Ruth did not say anything for a moment.

'Hannah.'

'Yes.'

'Why have you told me this.'

Hannah was quiet for a moment. She turned the cup once on the saucer.

'I told you on Thursday I do not photograph people any more.'

'Yes.'

'I photographed people for a publication in Johannesburg between forty-three and forty-six. I photographed mineworkers, mainly. Black mineworkers on the Reef. Strikers in forty-six. The photographs got the publication closed. They did not, however, get me into trouble of any consequence, because I am the sort of person I am. The photographs got two of the men in them killed. Not because of my photographs. They would have died anyway. But the photographs were among the things that were used in the trial. I did not know they would be used. I should have known. I did know, and I let myself not know, which is the same thing.' She set the cup down. 'I have made a decision since to be careful with people I become aware of. I am aware of you.'

Ruth held very still.

'Thank you,' she said.

'You do not need to thank me. I am asking you to be careful.'

'I will be careful.'

'Good.'

They sat for a while without speaking. The coffee was cooling. The biscuits had not been touched after Hannah's first one. Outside the tea-room window a tram ground past on its way down to the Foreshore and a man on a bicycle wove between two cars and disappeared up Adderley.

'There is one further thing,' Hannah said eventually. 'About the flat.'

'Yes.'

'You should not come for some weeks.'

Ruth looked at her.

'It is not because Clara would not want you to come. She would. But the warden has noticed your Thursday evenings and a Thursday evening in Brownlow Road is something the warden may, in time, attach a name to. Not now. In time. Clara will not mind. Clara would mind more if you came and lost your scholarship for it. The Tibullus may stay with you. Read it. Write to her about it through me, if you want. I see her on Tuesdays.'

She said this without inflection, without softening, as a piece of practical information.

Ruth nodded once. The nod did not feel like agreement, exactly. It was the gesture a person makes when they are receiving a thing that is being given to them and have understood that the gift is also the cost.

'I understand.'

'Good.' Hannah picked up the bill the waitress had left. 'My coffee. You may pay for the biscuits if you want a record of independence. Otherwise I will pay for them and you will owe me biscuits at some indeterminate future point.'

'Biscuits.'

'Biscuits. Now go back to your library. You should be seen in the library this morning. The librarians know who comes in and who does not. They are not, for our purposes, on our side or against us. They are inventory. But inventory matters.'

Ruth left the tea room ahead of Hannah and walked back up Adderley alone, the tripod-less satchel light against her side. The brown Tibullus and the green Cavafy were inside it. The two books knocked against each other faintly with each step.

She went back to the Jagger. She returned the green Cavafy to the upper gallery, replacing it precisely where it had been. The slot held the shape of the absence for the few seconds in which she set the book back, and then the absence was filled and the gallery was as it had been.

She went down to her carrel. She opened the brown Tibullus. She opened the notebook. She read the passage she had translated that morning. She read the two lines she had written underneath. She read them again. Then she turned to the next blank page of the notebook and began, in the same neat hand, a new translation of an entirely different elegy, the first elegy of the first book, which she had chosen for no reason except that it had nothing to do with Marathus.

She worked through the morning. The Latin held her. The work held her. The work was the only thing in the room that did not depend on what other people had decided about her.

At lectures that afternoon she sat in her usual place. Arbuthnot did not look at her. He read Sophocles, the passage on Antigone refusing to leave Polyneices unburied. He read it in Greek and in English and then closed the book and said that the question for the afternoon was not whether Antigone was right but whether the polis was capable, in any structure that had ever existed, of accommodating the kind of being who was prepared to die for the unburied. He asked the question without asking it of anyone in particular. He took notes from the row of doctoral students at the back. Ruth wrote nothing in her notebook. She listened.

She walked back up to the residence in the late afternoon along the path through the pines, the satchel against her side. The wind had not come up. The mountain was clear in the early-evening light, the rock face holding the last gold of the sun, the lower slopes already in shadow.

She passed Miss Pryce on the residence drive. Miss Pryce was speaking to one of the under-wardens, the clipboard against her chest. Miss Pryce looked up as Ruth passed, and Ruth said, 'Good evening, Warden,' and Miss Pryce said, 'Good evening, Miss MacLeod,' and they both moved on.

In Room 412 Ruth took off her coat and hung it on the hook on the door and sat on the edge of the bed.

She took the brown Tibullus out of the satchel and put it on the desk. She sat for a moment with her hands in her lap.

She wrote a letter then, on a single sheet of the residence's writing paper, headed *General Botha Hall, University of Cape Town,* in her neat hand. She did not address it. She wrote three lines.

*The window opens. The light may, as you said, be a new tyranny. I am attending Sunday tea this week.*

She did not sign it.

She folded it once and slid it into the brown Tibullus at the page of the second elegy of the third book and closed the cover.

She sat for a while looking at the volume on the desk. The light along the western wall had turned the colour of clean brass. The mountain was beginning to lose its definition above the residence roofline. The pines along the drive were moving in the slow, dry agreement of evening.

She did not light the lamp.

She picked up the green silk square from the desk, the one Judith had lent her on a Friday evening at the Cohens' three weeks before and that Ruth had not yet returned, and folded it once on her palm, and then folded it again, and held it in her lap.

She sat in the gathering dark with the square in her hand and did not move.

# Ch12 *Names for Hunger* — Full Rewrite Complete

**File written:** `Ch12_Names_for_Hunger-Humanized.txt` · **5,150 words** (rewritten from a 3,977-word AI draft)

The original Ch12_Expanded.txt is untouched. The humanized file is a clean ground-up rewrite respecting the canon bible.

## Decisions made within authorisation

- **(b) Eleanor Stratford-Reid debut — held for Ch13.** Eleanor's canon entry requires a major scene executing the immediate-engagement triad. Combining it with Pryce's first activation and the Cavafy/Tibullus solo discovery would overstuff Ch12. **Eleanor is named in Ch12** (Pryce: *'Eleanor Stratford-Reid is convening it'*) — establishing her presence as social weather before her on-page entrance. The Sunday tea is now planted as the Ch13 opening.
- **(d) Sarah held off-stage.** Sarah is referenced only in Ruth's interior taxonomy of registers (*'Not Sarah's measuring glance over the water carafe'*), preserving her independent arc.
- **(e) Hannah recurs as the golden-thread continuity character.** Hannah is the working-laconic photographer from Ch11; she becomes the protective intermediary who closes one loop (her admission about why she stopped photographing people) while opening another (the explicit naming of the conduct-report mechanism, the suspension from Brownlow Road, the agreed Tuesday correspondence channel). Clara is held off-stage and re-routed through Hannah, maintaining the canonical *recognition + peril together* register without staging a third Brownlow Road scene.
- **Cavafy "Windows" placed solo on the page** — Ruth finds the name for hunger alone in the Jagger upper gallery, not from Clara's mouth. The Mavrogordato 1948 Hogarth Press translation is real and period-correct.
- **Tibullus Marathus loop partially closed** — Ruth translates the second elegy of the third book and writes two lines underneath. The Lobeck edition with English notes is period-defensible. Arbuthnot's brief steps-of-the-Jagger remark *'I should not, were I you, allow myself to be disposed of by a textual question'* establishes him in the *benevolent gatekeeper* register canon requires.
- **Pryce activated within canon constraint** — administrative warmth, *Sunday tea convening*, *'I do not, as you know, keep a record. But I do notice.'* The mechanism engages without Ruth fully grasping the threat in real time. Hannah names it for her over coffee on St George's Mall.

## What this means

- The Expanded source for Ch13 is not "On the Mountain." It is a low-valence Afrikaner-nationalist drawing-room scene that does not exist in the canonical sequence.
- Several of the canon's required chapters have **no source draft at all** (On the Mountain, The City's Dark Grammar, Men After War, The Uncle at the Beach, Ink and Judgment, Rumour, Winter North, No Homecoming, What Remains of Family, To Krugersdorp Alone).
- Several source chapters belong to a discarded structure (The Boer House, The Return of the Relative, The Geography of Silence, The Architecture of Memory, The Language of Loss, The Final Translation, The Disciplinary Council, The Epilogue/After, The World Unmade).
- Ch11, Ch12, Ch19 happen to align by title only — and Ch11/Ch12 source were already substantively misaligned with canon (which is why both required full rewrites).

## Decision required

I need your authority on one of three paths before I touch another file:

**(A) Canon-first authoring.** Treat the chapter map as authority. From Ch13 onward I write each chapter ground-up against the canon bible and chapter-map titles, ignoring the Expanded source files entirely (or mining them only for stray period detail). This is the cleanest path; Ch11 and Ch12 were already effectively this. Output: ten new chapters of original prose authored to canon spec.

**(B) Source-first remediation.** Treat the Expanded files as authority. I humanize them in their existing sequence, which means accepting "The Boer House," "The Return of the Relative," etc. as the actual Ch13–Ch24, and updating the chapter map and canon bible to match. The valence curve (4-6-6-7-5-8-8-9-8-6-7-8-9 etc.) collapses. The mountain-climbing / fear-of-heights / humiliation-and-rebirth canon constraint is forfeited. The Ch22 summit moves.

**(C) Hybrid mapping.** I attempt to map each Expanded source onto the closest canon slot (e.g., "The Boer House" → cut or relocated as a Ch15 *Men After War* contrast piece; "The Architecture of Memory" Muizenberg seaside scene → repurposed as raw material for Ch16 *The Uncle at the Beach*). High labour, mixed result.

My recommendation: **(A)**. It is what we have effectively been doing for Ch11 and Ch12 already, and it is the only path that preserves the valence architecture, the Ch22 summit, and the canonical mountain-as-rebirth structural beat.

State your choice and I will proceed.

## Ch13 Phase A — Structural Brief

**Canon obligations:**
- Valence 9 (first peak); physical terror + humiliating exposure + rebirth
- Ruth has a severe fear of heights — canon-established trait, never previously dramatised
- Near-death experience must become both humiliation *and* rebirth
- Forward-locked from Ch12: Ruth attends Sunday tea convened by Eleanor Stratford-Reid; complies with Hannah's instruction (attend Sunday tea, attend chapel, suspend Brownlow Road)

**Three-movement structure:**
1. **Sunday tea** (Hiddingh Hall common room, residence): Eleanor's debut. Cigarette case. Walking party announced.
2. **The mountain** (Saturday morning, late April 1949): Kloof Nek tea-room rendezvous; ascent of Platteklip Gorge; deviation onto an exposed traverse; the freeze.
3. **The descent** (Ruth's own movement; Connie returning; tram back; residence corridor): rebirth through precision; humiliation re-staged as social currency.

**Open Zeigarnik objects threaded:** brown Tibullus + unsigned three-line letter (still folded inside, unsent); green silk square (still not returned to Judith); Pryce conduct-report mechanism (now extending its reach); Sunday tea precedent now established.

# Chapter 13: On the Mountain

The common room on the first floor of General Botha Hall was a room of polished ox-blood linoleum and tall sash windows that admitted the late autumn afternoon at a slant. The chairs were arranged around two low tables in the manner of a furniture catalogue. There was a piano against the long wall that no one played. Ruth sat in the armchair nearest the door because she had learnt in the convent that the seat nearest the door was the seat that allowed her to read a room without being read.

She had attended chapel that morning in the third row as Hannah had told her to. She had eaten at the long table with twenty-two other girls and Miss Pryce had nodded to her at the head of the room. She had crossed the courtyard in a cold clean autumn light and climbed the stairs to her room and changed her stockings and put on the navy wool dress because the wool dress was the only dress she owned that did not register strain.

It was four o'clock. The Sunday tea was a fixture. The fixture had a convenor.

The convenor was already there when Ruth came in, standing at the window with a cup and saucer in her left hand, the cup balanced on the saucer without touching it. She was tall, fair, in a fawn skirt and a cream blouse with a collar that had been ironed by someone other than her. The pearls were not at her throat. The pearls were a light double row at her wrist, looped twice, which Ruth had not seen before and which seemed in some way more accurate to the woman than the throat-pearls would have been.

'Miss MacLeod,' she said, without turning her head. 'Come and have tea.'

Ruth crossed the room. The room registered her crossing. There were six other girls already seated, three in the chairs by the piano, three around the further table, and they did not look up as she passed but they did make a small adjustment in their postures. Connie Hargreaves was one of the three by the piano, in a grey skirt and a pale blue cardigan, her ankles crossed. Vivienne Hartley was at the further table, leaning toward another girl, laughing at something Ruth had not heard.

'I'm Eleanor Stratford-Reid,' the woman at the window said. She turned then, and Ruth saw her face properly for the first time, the broad pale forehead, the eyes a flat colour that the afternoon light did not assist, the mouth narrow and well-shaped and entirely under instruction. 'I don't think we have actually been introduced. I've seen you in the dining hall. You sit in the corner with the girl from the sciences.'

'Miriam,' Ruth said.

'Miriam,' Eleanor said, and gave the name back without comment, as one gives back a passport at a border. 'She is reading what?'

'Botany.'

'How nice for her. Tea?'

'Thank you.'

Eleanor poured. Her hand was steady on the spout. The tea fell in a thin amber line. Ruth took the cup and the saucer and held the saucer against her palm and felt the heat through the china. She had walked into the room intending to drink the tea standing because standing kept the body alert, and then she had remembered Hannah on the bench at St George's Mall saying *attend the Sunday tea this week* and she had sat.

She sat.

'You are reading classics,' Eleanor said, without making it a question.

'Latin, Greek, ancient history, English.'

'A heavy load for the first year.'

'It is the standard load for a scholarship.'

Eleanor's mouth made a brief private movement that was not quite a smile. 'You answer like a man who has been asked the question before.'

'I have.'

'Yes. I expect you have.' She set her own cup down on the windowsill and looked at Ruth from a height she did not need to construct. 'Professor Arbuthnot is teaching Latin one this year, I think.'

'Yes.'

'My father knew his uncle in Stellenbosch in the twenties. The classics in this country are a small profession.' She paused. The pause was long enough to be noticed and short enough not to be remarked upon. 'I wanted to ask you something. We are walking the mountain on Saturday. A small group, my brother and a friend of his and Constance and Vivienne, going up Platteklip and down by the cableway. I wondered whether you would come.'

Ruth set her cup down carefully on the low table. The cup was not quite level on the saucer and she made the saucer level and then set it down. The room was not louder than it had been but it had become more attentive. Connie's ankles by the piano had not uncrossed but the angle of her head had altered.

'I have not walked the mountain.'

'No, I gathered. That is why I asked. It is a pleasant walk. Three hours up, an hour at the top, fifteen minutes down by the car. We have a thermos and sandwiches.'

'I would like to come,' Ruth said, and the saying was a calculation made in a fraction of a second and the calculation included Hannah on the bench and the light moving on the water and *attend the Sunday tea this week*. There was no Sunday tea after this one to which the answer would not be the answer it was now. 'Thank you.'

'Saturday at eight, then. The tram from the residence at half past seven gets you to Kloof Nek by quarter to eight. There is a tea-room. We meet there.' Eleanor reached, then, into the small leather bag at her hip and withdrew a flat object the size of a deck of cards and the colour of brushed brass. She opened it with one thumb. The inside was tooled gold, the cigarettes laid in a precise overlapping fan. There was an engraved monogram at the inside of the lid that Ruth could not read at the distance. Eleanor took out a cigarette and tapped it on the back of her left hand and closed the case again with the same thumb. She did not light the cigarette. She held it.

'You should wear something warmer than what you have on now,' she said. 'There is wind on the upper slopes even in April.'

'I have an overcoat.'

'A coat is too heavy for the climb. A jersey under your jacket is sufficient.'

'I will wear a jersey.'

'Good.' The cigarette returned to the case. The case returned to the bag. The whole movement had taken perhaps eleven seconds and Ruth had read every second of it. 'I am pleased you can come.'

It was not pleasure. It was the noting of an event.

Vivienne came over from the further table with her own cup. 'Miss MacLeod is joining us on Saturday,' Eleanor said, as Vivienne arrived.

'How lovely,' Vivienne said. Her voice carried the ringing quality of a woman who had been told she had a lovely voice and had practised on the telling. 'Have you walked Platteklip before?'

'No.'

'Oh, you mustn't worry. Hugh always carries the thermos and Robin is the slowest of us. We accommodate the slowest.' She looked sidelong at Eleanor with the small private gleam of an ally. 'It is not an athletic event. It is the social meaning of the event that is the event.'

'I see,' Ruth said. She did, and Vivienne saw that she did.

'Good,' said Eleanor.

Connie crossed the room then, her cup empty, and stopped beside Eleanor's chair without sitting. 'Saturday is the day before the Goldsworthy ball,' she said to Eleanor.

'I know.'

'The Sunday after a Saturday on the mountain is rather a great deal of standing.'

'It is for some of us,' Eleanor said, without turning her head. 'Miss MacLeod, you do not happen to be invited to the Goldsworthy ball, I should think.'

'No.'

'You will recover the more easily, then. Constance, would you sit. You are looming.'

Connie sat on the edge of the chair next to Eleanor's, her ankles re-crossing in the new position. She gave Ruth the brief speculative glance that Ruth recognised from the dining hall, the glance that took in the wool dress and the school shoes and the hands that had not been creamed since September. The glance was speculative because Connie was working the calculation and the calculation was not quite resolved. Connie set the cup down on the table between them with a faint chime of china against china that was not quite a sound she had intended.

'You are very kind to invite me,' Ruth said.

'I am not,' said Eleanor. 'I simply believed you would want to see the mountain from the top before the rains start.'

The mountain stood in the upper sash of the window above Eleanor's right shoulder. Its upper face was the colour of dry stone and dust. The southern shoulder caught the late light. The cableway cabin was a small distant rectangle on the wire, ascending. Ruth had looked at the mountain every day since January and had not been told until that minute that she would be on it.

She drank the tea.

When she rose to leave the room twenty minutes later, having spoken twice more about nothing, having registered every chair, having calculated her exit, Vivienne was already telling a story to Connie about a girl in their year who had been taken off the books, and Eleanor was at the window again, with the cigarette case in her hand, half open, and the cigarette still unlit. The afternoon light had begun to go.

In the corridor outside the common room, Miss Pryce was speaking to one of the kitchen girls about the linen. She did not turn her head as Ruth passed. She raised one hand slightly in the small administrative gesture by which she registered the passing of any girl in her care.

In Room 412 Ruth sat on the bed and did not take off her shoes.

She had agreed to walk up a mountain.

The window had begun to lose the light along the upper line of the residence roofline. The pines in the drive were moving in the same slow dry agreement they had been moving in on Friday evening when she had folded the silk square in her lap. The brown Tibullus was on the desk where she had left it. The folded letter was still inside it at the page of the second elegy.

She had agreed to walk up a mountain because Hannah had told her to attend the Sunday tea. The Sunday tea had brought the mountain. The mountain had been there all along.

She got up and went to the small chest of drawers and opened the second drawer down. Inside the drawer were her two jerseys, neither of them new. She took out the heavier one, the dark grey one her mother had knitted in the year before she left for the convent, and laid it across the foot of the bed. She took off her shoes. She placed the shoes side by side under the chair.

She sat down again on the edge of the bed and looked at the jersey on the counterpane and did not move for some time.

\*

Saturday came up cold.

The tram from the residence to Kloof Nek was empty at twenty past seven of any other woman travelling the mountain road. Ruth sat in the back seat with the jersey on under her tweed jacket and the wool dress under the jersey and her hands inside her coat pockets, and watched Cape Town tilt sideways as the line climbed. The mountain was not yet in sun. The lower slopes were wet from a night of mist. The pines along the road carried the smell of their own resin.

The tea-room at Kloof Nek was a small white wooden building with a tin roof and a verandah. There were two motor cars parked on the gravel and a man in walking boots was standing by the further one, smoking. He was perhaps twenty-three. He was tall and angular and carried himself with the unhurried looseness of a man who had been told from boyhood that the earth was waiting for him to step on it. He looked up as Ruth approached.

'You will be Miss MacLeod,' he said.

'I am.'

'Hugh Stratford-Reid. Eleanor's cousin.' He held out a hand. The hand was warm. 'They are inside arguing about the thermos. Would you go in.'

She went in. The inside of the tea-room was dim and smelled of black tea and paraffin. Eleanor was at a small round table with a young man Ruth had not met, both of them turned slightly away from a folded canvas knapsack in the middle of the table. Connie was at the counter paying for sandwiches in greaseproof paper. Vivienne was sitting on the verandah with her ankles crossed and her face turned toward the morning light.

'Ruth,' said Eleanor, looking up. The use of the first name was a marked event. 'This is Robin Hayes-Whittaker. Robin, Miss MacLeod. Robin reads law. Robin is the slowest of us today. He has eaten too much breakfast.'

'I have not,' Robin said amiably. He had a wide good face and an engineering of the mouth that promised to laugh at any reasonable invitation. 'How do you do.'

'How do you do.'

'Eleanor has filled the thermos with what she insists is coffee. I am filing a protest.'

'Sit on it,' Eleanor said. 'We are leaving in three minutes.'

In the event they left in seven. Hugh paid for two more rounds of sandwiches. Connie came out with the parcel of greaseproof and put it into the canvas knapsack on the table and then took the knapsack onto her own shoulders without asking, which Ruth saw Eleanor notice and not remark upon. Vivienne came in from the verandah with her face arranged. They walked out into the gravel and across the road and onto the path through the lower pines and the path was wide enough for two and Eleanor walked beside Robin and Hugh walked beside Vivienne and Connie walked behind with the knapsack and Ruth walked beside Connie because the geometry of six on a path of two had no other arithmetic.

The path went up.

The lower section of Platteklip Gorge is not strictly speaking a climb. It is a long stair of stone and gravel. The angle is steady and the breath has time to lengthen and the city falls away at the right shoulder by degrees. Ruth had not walked anything like it. She had walked the slope behind the Letaba farm as a child and she had walked the road from the convent into Pietersburg, but she had not climbed against gravity for an hour at a stretch and her legs had not been asked the question. She felt the question begin to be asked at twenty minutes. She felt it asked properly at forty.

The party stretched. Robin and Hugh moved ahead. Eleanor and Vivienne were a few yards behind. Connie was steady but slow with the weight, and Ruth was beside Connie, and Connie had not spoken since they entered the path.

'Are you well,' Connie said at fifty minutes, when Ruth had paused without intending to and had set her hand against a boulder to take a breath.

'I am well.'

'It is a different kind of effort from reading.'

'It is.'

'I find it makes the reading easier. The body calms down for a day after.' Connie looked up at the line of the gorge above them, at the steep grey throat of it where the path narrowed to a single file of stone. 'We are nearly through the lower section. The middle is the harder part. The upper is the easier. Eleanor has done it eleven times.'

'You have done it.'

'Six.'

'Six is not nothing.'

'It is not eleven.'

Connie's voice was even. She was not unkind. Her unkindness was already in the calculation she had finished about Ruth and the calculation had nothing to do with the present exchange. She started up again and Ruth followed her and Ruth's left calf had begun a small pulse of warning that she recognised from the corridor.

The middle section of Platteklip is a stone corridor between two walls of rock. At points the stone is wet from a thin seep that comes down the western face all year. The path bends and the angle steepens and the city below disappears for long minutes behind the bend and then reappears. The wind did not enter the corridor. The air in the corridor was still and warm and the warmth was the warmth of a body moving, not of a sun.

Ruth kept her eyes on the path two paces ahead of her boots and counted boots. At a hundred boots she paused. At a hundred more she paused. Connie went ahead. Robin and Hugh were entirely out of sight. Eleanor's voice came down once around a bend, calling something to Vivienne, and Vivienne laughed.

Ruth came round the upper bend of the corridor and the corridor ended and the world opened.

It opened like a cut.

The path debouched onto a wide flat scree at the upper end of the gorge, with the back of the mountain spreading away to the south in a long low plateau of grey rock and dry vegetation, and the city unrolled below to the right at a depth and a distance that Ruth could not, in the first second, accept. The drop along the right edge of the path was not protected. It was not fenced. It was the upper rim of the gorge falling away in a single grey throat to the city far below, and the city was not a city at the depth she saw it. It was a flat pattern of small streets and small roofs.

Her body knew before her mind. Her body had known on the slope behind the Letaba farm as a child, when she had climbed once to a krantz to see whether she could and could not, and her father had come up after her and told her quietly to sit, and she had sat for an hour before she could be carried down. Her body knew now in the same place it had known then, in the small region behind the breastbone where the lungs become unreasonable.

She did not move.

The party were a hundred yards across the upper plateau, gathered in a loose group where the path turned south toward the upper cableway station. Hugh was pointing at something. Robin was laughing. Eleanor was looking out toward the sea. Connie had stopped halfway across, the knapsack still on her back, her head turned, and she was watching Ruth.

'Come along, Ruth,' Eleanor called, without turning her head.

Ruth could not.

She could not lift her foot. The foot was not a foot. The foot was a weight that belonged to the rock and the rock belonged to the depth and the depth was not a thing she could think against. The wind, which she had not felt in the corridor, came across the plateau in a low cold drag that pulled at the hem of her coat and then lifted it and dropped it, and the lifting of the hem was the precise wrong sensation, the sensation of being pulled by something larger than her body toward the edge.

She closed her eyes.

She opened them.

The drop was still there. The drop was at the centre of her vision now even when she looked at the path. The path had become a line beside the drop and the line was narrower than it had been a moment ago.

'Ruth,' Eleanor called, more sharply.

Ruth heard the sharpness as if from the bottom of a glass. The sharpness was a piece of information about Eleanor. Eleanor was being looked at by Hugh and Robin and Vivienne and Eleanor required Ruth to move. Ruth understood this in the absolute clarity that her body was now manufacturing in place of motion. Fear had laid its hand on every detail of the plateau and was pressing down. The plateau was being measured. The measurement was very precise. The way the wind moved a particular tuft of grey grass at the lip of the drop. The way the line of the path had a small pale wear pattern in its centre where ten thousand boots had ground the stone smoother than the stone beside it. The way Connie's knapsack had a leather buckle at the lower strap that had not been quite done up.

She could not move.

Vivienne's voice came thinly from the further group. 'What is the matter with her.'

'Nothing,' Eleanor said, and the *nothing* was a public correction of Vivienne, not an answer about Ruth. 'She is taking it in.'

'She is not moving.'

'Vivienne.'

Connie had not moved either. Connie was standing in the middle of the plateau with the knapsack on her back, watching Ruth, and the watching was a calculation. Ruth could not at that distance read the calculation. Ruth could read only that Connie had not yet moved toward her and had not yet moved away.

The wind dropped. The hem of her coat went still. The stillness was a small mercy.

She thought, do not look down.

She had been looking down.

She moved her eyes very slowly along the path until the path went into the grey of the plateau and she stopped them there. The drop was at the side of her vision and not the centre. She could keep it at the side. She kept it at the side.

She thought, the foot.

The foot had not changed. The foot was still a weight.

She thought, the other foot. She would move the other foot. The left foot. The left foot was the foot further from the drop. If she moved the left foot first, the drop did not change.

She moved the left foot.

It moved an inch.

She moved it again. It moved an inch.

She did not look at the drop. She did not look at the others. She looked at the small wear pattern on the path. The wear pattern had a faint rust mark at one place where someone's boot nail had bled iron into the stone. She looked at the rust mark. She moved the left foot.

The right foot followed.

She had moved twelve inches.

She thought, twelve inches in a minute is sixty feet in an hour. She thought, the plateau is a hundred yards. She thought, the cableway station is three hundred yards. She thought, do not multiply.

She moved the left foot.

Behind her on the path, in the upper bend of the corridor, the wind that had dropped on the plateau was beginning again as a thin sound in the rock. The sound was a thin high whistle. She used the whistle. The whistle was behind her and the whistle was steady and the whistle did not belong to the drop.

Connie was moving.

Connie was walking back toward her across the plateau, the knapsack on her back, the strap she had not done up swinging slightly as she came. She was not hurrying. She was walking at the steady pace of a woman who had decided to come back and had not decided what she would say when she got there.

Ruth moved the left foot. The right foot followed. She had moved two feet.

Connie arrived. She stopped four feet from Ruth, on the path, between Ruth and the further group.

'Ruth.'

Ruth did not look up from the rust mark on the stone.

'I am alright.'

'You are not.'

'I am moving.'

'Yes.' Connie's voice had gone very neutral. The neutrality was a kindness. The neutrality was also an exhibit. Ruth understood, in the same precise place where she had understood the wind, that Connie's neutrality would later be reported. 'May I take your arm.'

'No.'

'You are sure.'

'Yes.'

Connie did not press. She turned her body slightly to put herself between Ruth and the drop, which was the older courtesy a horseman gave to a horse, and stood there. She did not speak again for a minute.

'I would like you to walk in front of me,' Ruth said, after the minute. Her voice came out exact. The voice surprised her by being exact. 'I do not want anyone behind me.'

'Yes,' Connie said.

Connie turned and walked very slowly along the path toward the further group. Ruth followed. She moved the left foot. The right foot followed. The drop was still at the side of her vision. The drop was the same drop. She was moving along beside it. She would move along beside it for as long as the drop was there.

She made the plateau.

She did not look up until she was past the place where the further group had been standing and the path began to descend toward the upper cableway station, and when she looked up the further group were a way ahead, walking, not looking back. Eleanor's fawn coat moved against the grey of the upper rock with a small precise rhythm. Hugh's hand was on Vivienne's elbow. Robin was lighting a cigarette in the lee of a boulder.

Connie was a yard ahead of Ruth, walking with the knapsack.

Ruth did not look at the drop again.

\*

At the upper cableway station Eleanor had bought a cup of coffee from the small kiosk and was standing at the wall with her cup. She did not turn as Ruth and Connie came up. She did turn after a measured count, and when she turned her face was arranged in the particular composure she used in the dining hall when an under-warden had said something that did not require an answer.

'You are alright,' she said to Ruth.

'Yes.'

'The first time on the upper slopes is sometimes a surprise.'

'Yes.'

'Hugh has tickets for the cabin down. The cabin leaves in eight minutes.'

'Thank you.'

'Constance, you carried the knapsack. I am sorry. I should have taken a turn.'

'It was not heavy,' Connie said.

'Vivienne tells me you helped Ruth up the last of it.'

'A little.'

'Kind of you.'

Connie did not answer. She unbuckled the lower strap of the knapsack with a small movement and re-did it. Eleanor watched the movement and turned back to her cup.

Ruth went to the wall. The wall was a low parapet of dressed stone built when the cableway had first been put up twenty years before, and it was the first real wall she had seen all morning and her hands took it without instruction. She put her hands flat on the stone. The stone was cold. She pressed her hands down on the cold stone and she stood at the wall and she did not look out.

The cabin came up the wire.

The cabin was a small round metal room with windows on every side. It descended by sliding down the sustaining wire from the upper station to the lower station in seven minutes. There were eleven other people in the cabin and there was a man at the front who operated it with a single brass lever. Ruth went to the centre of the cabin and stood with her back against the central column and she put a hand on the column and did not move from it.

The cabin descended. The cabin swayed in the cross-wind at the second pylon. A girl beside Ruth gave a short small laugh of pleasure at the sway. Ruth's hand on the column was the only thing in the cabin that was not pleasure.

At the lower station Ruth got out and walked across the gravel to the road and stood on the road in the sun. Eleanor came out behind her and did not look at her. Hugh came out and lit another cigarette. Robin came out laughing about something.

Connie came out last. Connie set the knapsack down on the gravel and unbuckled the lid and took out the greaseproof parcel of sandwiches and unwrapped them on the lid of the knapsack as though it were a tray.

'There are six,' she said. 'Two cheese, two egg, two ham. Help yourselves.'

They ate the sandwiches on the gravel by the road.

Vivienne came over to Ruth as they ate, and stood beside her, and said, in a voice pitched not quite low enough, 'Are you sure you would not rather sit, Ruth. We could go to the tea-room.'

'I am alright.'

'You looked rather frightened up there.'

'It was the wind.'

'Was it.'

'Yes.'

Vivienne laughed her practised laugh. The laugh meant that Vivienne accepted what Ruth had said as a passable account that she would convert later. 'The wind on the upper plateau is a brute. I cried the first time. Eleanor was unbearable about it for weeks.'

'I am sure.'

Vivienne moved closer and lowered her voice, and the voice when it came out lowered was softer than Ruth had heard it. 'You did very well, you know. Coming back across like that. You did rather better than a number of girls would have done.'

For half a second the softness was real. Ruth could hear that it was real. Vivienne, who had been a polished surface all morning, had said something that came from a less polished place. The surface returned a second later and Vivienne stepped back, and the next thing she said, called over her shoulder to Eleanor, was *Robin has eaten the last egg sandwich* in the calibrated voice of the morning, and the moment was gone.

Ruth filed it. The softness had been there. The softness would be useful later if she remembered exactly that it had occurred.

The tram down was at half past one. Eleanor said her father's car was meeting them at the foot of Kloof Nek and would take them as far as Sea Point and Ruth was welcome to come if she liked, and Ruth said no thank you, the tram would do, and Eleanor said as you wish in the voice that registered the refusal as data. The party walked down the mountain road to the tram stop together and at the tram stop Hugh shook Ruth's hand again and Robin said *Good show, Miss MacLeod*, and Eleanor said *I am pleased you came*, and Connie said nothing and Vivienne said *We must do it again when the rains stop*. The car came. The four of them got into it. Connie got into the back seat and did up the seat belt and did not look at Ruth through the window.

Ruth waited at the tram stop.

The tram came. She got in. She sat in the back seat. The tram descended the mountain road to the city and the city laid itself out as it had when she had ridden up that morning and the city was the same city. The mountain was behind her now. She did not turn to look at it.

She looked at her hands instead. Her hands were on her knees. The knuckles were white. She made the knuckles unwhiten one finger at a time.

\*

In the residence Miss Pryce was in the lower hall when Ruth came in. She was conferring with the head matron about a delivery. She looked up as Ruth crossed the hall.

'Miss MacLeod.'

'Warden.'

'You have been on the mountain.'

'Yes.'

'With Miss Stratford-Reid's party.'

'Yes.'

'I trust the day was a success.'

'Yes, thank you.'

'Good.' Miss Pryce did not say anything else. She nodded once and turned back to the matron, and the conferring continued about the linen, and Ruth went up the stairs.

In Room 412 Ruth took off the tweed jacket and hung it over the back of the chair. She took off the heavy grey jersey and folded it on the bed. She sat on the bed in the wool dress with her hands in her lap.

After some time she got up and went to the desk and opened the brown Tibullus to the page of the second elegy of the third book, and the folded sheet of writing paper was still inside it where she had left it on Friday evening. She took the sheet out. She unfolded it. She read the three lines.

*The window opens. The light may, as you said, be a new tyranny. I am attending Sunday tea this week.*

She took up the pen.

Below the three lines, in the same neat hand, she wrote a fourth.

*I went up the mountain on Saturday and could not move at the top.*

She read the fourth line.

She did not sign the letter.

She folded it again along the same fold and slid it back into the Tibullus at the same page and closed the cover.

She picked up the green silk square from the desk and folded it once on her palm and folded it again and laid it across her knee.

The light along the western wall had gone the colour of clean brass, as it had done on Friday and would do on every clear Sunday evening of the autumn. The mountain was beginning to lose its definition above the residence roofline. The pines along the drive were moving in their slow dry agreement of evening.

She did not light the lamp.

She sat with the square folded on her knee and her hands open on the counterpane and she did not move for a long while, and when she did move, it was only to put her right hand flat against her own breastbone, where the lungs had become unreasonable on the upper plateau, and to leave it there.

The breath was steady.

The breath had been steady, she realised, since the wall at the cableway station.

She kept her hand where it was.

## Structural decisions executed

- **Three movements:** Sunday tea (Eleanor's debut + walking party arranged) → Saturday on Platteklip (ascent + freeze) → descent + cableway + tram + residence (rebirth through precision).
- **Eleanor's introduction triad** executed: she shows trained self-command (cup balanced on saucer without touching), intelligence under pressure (the Stellenbosch genealogy probe of Arbuthnot), visibly inside a problem before scene settles (the Goldsworthy-ball / mountain-walk arithmetic with Connie).
- **Cigarette case canonised** — brushed brass, gold-tooled interior, monogrammed, opened-and-closed without lighting; pearls re-routed to the wrist (more accurate to her than throat-pearls).
- **Connie's mechanism** activated: studied graciousness as concern; returns to Ruth on the plateau, frames the rescue as not-a-rescue, but the framing is itself the report mechanism.
- **Vivienne's permitted moment of warmth** delivered: the *you did rather better than a number of girls would have done* aside, unguarded for half a second, then the surface returns. Ruth files it explicitly: *the softness would be useful later if she remembered exactly that it had occurred*. This loop is now open for Vivienne's later betrayal.
- **Ruth's fear-of-heights canon** anchored to the Letaba escarpment childhood memory (krantz; her father carried her down) — establishes that this is not new fear, it is body-memory.
- **Rebirth through precision, not rescue:** Ruth begins moving herself before Connie reaches her. The fear makes her precise: she thins the world to the rust-mark on the path, the wear-pattern, the unbuckled strap. The summit-canon signature *fear makes her precise* drives the descent. Connie arrives to walk in front of her, not to take her arm.
- **Pryce mechanism continues quietly:** "You have been on the mountain. With Miss Stratford-Reid's party." The administrative warmth notes the social registration; Ruth has been registered.
- **Closing arrest:** Ruth in Room 412, the silk square folded on her knee, her hand on her own breastbone where the lungs went unreasonable on the plateau, the breath now steady. No commentary. The fourth line added under the unsigned letter — *I went up the mountain on Saturday and could not move at the top* — replaces aphorism with quiet documentary.
